13:I[27633,["619","static/chunks/619-f072ac750404f9da.js","909","static/chunks/909-df1df79f44c4b47c.js","499","static/chunks/app/chapters/%5Bchapter%5D/page-d164dc85b7b8428a.js"],"ChapterStudy"]
14:T53e,The biblical evidence points to papal Rome:
1. It comes from the fourth beast, which Daniel 7:23 identifies as the fourth kingdom and Daniel 2 has already shown to be Rome.
2. It appears among the ten horns, placing it in the territory of divided Western Rome rather than Asia, Africa, or the Greek period.
3. It rises after the ten horns are already present, so its decisive rise follows the breakup of Western Rome after A.D. 476.
4. It uproots three horns; the Heruli, Vandals, and Ostrogoths were removed as papal supremacy gained room to stand.
5. It becomes more stout than its fellows, growing from small beginnings into a power able to influence kings and nations.
6. It is diverse from the first horns, because it is religious-political rather than merely civil.
7. It has eyes like a man and a mouth speaking great things, pointing to human wisdom exalted above Scripture and blasphemous claims about divine authority.
8. It makes war on the saints and wears them out, matching the long history of persecution under coercive church-state power.
9. It thinks to change times and law, reaching toward the sphere of worship, conscience, and God's commandments.
10. It rules for a time, times, and half a time, the 1260-day prophetic period also expressed in Revelation as forty-two months or 1260 days and fulfilled from A.D. 538-1798.15:T53e,The biblical evidence points to papal Rome:

10. It rules for a time, times, and half a time, the 1260-day prophetic period also expressed in Revelation as forty-two months or 1260 days and fulfilled from A.D. 538-1798.18:T4a6,The verse gives the later actions and the time limit in numbered clarity:
1. It speaks great words against the Most High, which Scripture defines as blasphemy when a human claims divine status or authority to forgive sins.
2. It wears out the saints, so the issue is persecution of God's people, not merely disagreement over ideas.
3. It thinks to change times, reaching into what belongs to God, who alone rules times and seasons.
4. It thinks to change law, especially in relation to worship, obedience, and the commandments.
5. The saints are given into its hand, showing that God permits a limited period of oppression without surrendering final control.
6. The period is time, times, and half a time, or three and a half prophetic years.
7. Revelation restates the same period as forty-two months and 1260 days.
8. Numbers 14:34 and Ezekiel 4:6 give the biblical year-day principle, so the period represents 1260 years.
9. The dates A.D. 538-1798 fit the rise and historical wound of papal supremacy, ending with the papal captivity under Berthier.
10. Antiochus IV does not satisfy the Roman setting, the post-A.D. 476 timing, the law-changing claim, and the 1260-year scope together.19:T4a6,The verse gives the later actions and the time limit in numbered clarity:

Chronologically, this chapter happens before the feast of Daniel 5 and before Daniel's service under Darius in Daniel 6, even though it appears after the court stories. The book now deliberately moves from the narratives of Daniel 1-6 into the prophetic visions of Daniel 7-12.","The date matters because Daniel is still living under the first empire in the sequence. Babylon's prophetic dominance runs from 605-539 B.C., beginning with Nebuchadnezzar's first invasion of Jerusalem and ending when Medo-Persia takes Babylon. Belshazzar's feast in Daniel 5 has not happened yet, but God already shows Daniel the whole sweep of empire, judgment, and kingdom.","Daniel writes the dream and tells the sum of the matters. Prophecy is not treated as a private impression to be enjoyed and forgotten; it is preserved for God's people. The faithful in exile need more than courage for today. They need to know that the God who gave Judah into Babylon's hand is also the God who will judge every beastly power.","This setting also shows why Daniel 7 is placed where it is. The reader has just watched faithful people survive Babylonian education, the image on the plain, the pride of Nebuchadnezzar, the fall of Babylon, and the lions' den. Now the curtain pulls back. The stories have shown how God's people live under empire; the vision explains why those empires are temporary and why faithfulness is worth the cost.","Belshazzar's first year is also pastorally important. Babylon had not yet fallen. From Daniel's visible position, the empire still looked established. Yet God reveals Babylon's end before the banquet of Daniel 5 ever happens. Prophecy is given so faith can see beyond the confidence of the present age.","The language of the chapter also matters. Daniel 7 stands near the end of the Aramaic section of the book, which runs from Daniel 2:4b through 7:28 and has a broad world-empire focus. The vision therefore speaks not only to Judah's private hopes, but to the public story of nations. God is explaining the world stage to an exile who has already served inside Babylon's court.","This is a steadying place to begin. When the present empire still looks permanent, God can already reveal its end. Faith learns to live responsibly inside history without mistaking any kingdom for the final word."],"explanation":"Daniel 7 opens in the first year of Belshazzar, before Babylon fell in 539 B.C. The picture is restless from the start. Wind, water, darkness, and conflict tell the reader that the kingdoms about to appear do not rise out of peace but out of the agitation of nations.","Scripture gives the symbols their meaning. Revelation 17:15 interprets waters as peoples, multitudes, nations, and tongues. Isaiah 17:12-13 compares nations to the rushing of many waters, and Isaiah 57:20 pictures the wicked as a troubled sea. Winds in prophetic language often mark scattering, strife, judgment, or restraint, as in Jeremiah 49:36-37 and Revelation 7:1-3.","The sea, then, is not a vague image for mystery. It is the world of peoples and nations out of which successive empires arise. Daniel is seeing world history from heaven's point of view. What looks impressive in royal inscriptions looks turbulent in prophecy, because power separated from God becomes unstable, violent, and proud.","The four winds are not random weather. They belong to the symbolic world of prophecy where political powers are shaken, restrained, released, and judged. Jeremiah 49:36 speaks of four winds scattering Elam. Revelation 7:1 pictures four angels holding back the winds so destructive forces do not break loose before God's servants are sealed. Daniel is seeing nations in motion under forces larger than diplomacy.","The great sea matters just as much. Revelation 17:15 gives the interpretive key by saying waters represent peoples, multitudes, nations, and tongues. That does not mean Daniel had Revelation in front of him; it means the Bible's prophetic language is consistent. Empires rise out of humanity's restless political sea, not from the calm of righteousness. Daniel's world is not controlled by chance currents; the sea is troubled, but God is already moving the vision toward His throne.","The four winds coming from heaven also keep the vision from becoming fatalistic. Nations move, armies rise, governments shake, and peoples are stirred, but the winds are not independent gods. Daniel sees turmoil under heaven's supervision. That is why the same chapter can describe violent waters and still move calmly to a throne. The storm is real, but it is not ultimate.","This symbol gives readers a biblical way to think about history. Political upheaval is not proof that God has lost control. It is often the very setting in which prophecy reveals His control most clearly. Daniel does not invite God's people to trust the sea, but to trust the God who can show what rises from it and where it ends.","The comfort is that the winds and sea do not rule the chapter. The vision will move from the troubled waters to the throne of the Ancient of Days. History may feel storm-driven, but Daniel places the storm under the eye of God."],"explanation":"Daniel sees the vision by night: the four winds of heaven strive upon the great sea. What looks impressive in royal inscriptions looks turbulent in prophecy, because power separated from God becomes unstable, violent, and proud.","sourceProfile":"$undefined","sourceAudit":"$undefined","confidence":"medium","sources":[],"reviewStatus":"source-backed"},{"verse":3,"reference":"Daniel 7:3","bibleText":"And four great beasts came up from the sea, diverse one from another.","detailedCommentary":["Four great beasts come up from the sea, each different from the others. Daniel has already seen the sequence of kingdoms in chapter 2 as metals in an image; now he sees the same line of history through beasts. The change of symbol matters. Earth may admire the splendor of empire, but heaven exposes its predatory character.","The angel later explains that the beasts are kings or kingdoms. Daniel 7:17 says the four beasts are four kings, and Daniel 7:23 clarifies that the fourth beast is a kingdom. Daniel 8 confirms this method by naming the ram as Medo-Persia and the goat as Greece. The Bible itself interprets beasts as ruling powers.","The four beasts correspond to Babylon, Medo-Persia, Greece, and Rome. The prophetic dominance dates are Babylon 605-539 B.C., Medo-Persia 539-331 B.C., Greece 331-168 B.C., and Rome 168 B.C.-A.D. 476. After Rome's western fragmentation, divided Europe becomes the setting in which the little horn rises.","Daniel 2 and Daniel 7 should be read together. Daniel 2 shows the kingdoms as metals in a man-shaped image, the way empire likes to present itself: glorious, ordered, impressive, and valuable. Daniel 7 shows the same line as beasts, the way heaven unmasks power when it becomes proud, violent, and predatory. The two chapters are not competing outlines; they are two angles on the same history.","The sequence is also expanded later in Daniel. Daniel 8 names Medo-Persia and Greece directly, so the reader is not left guessing. Rome is identified by its place as the fourth power after Greece and by its iron-like crushing character matching Daniel 2. The chapter trains us to let Scripture build the interpretation step by step rather than forcing symbols to fit private imagination.","The order of the beasts also protects the reader from a purely symbolic reading that floats away from history. Daniel is not speaking only about evil in general. He is tracing a succession. Daniel 2 gives the metal image, Daniel 7 gives the beast image, Daniel 8 names two of the kingdoms, and Daniel 11 follows the later struggles in greater detail. Prophecy is moral, but it is also historical.","At the same time, the beast imagery keeps the dates from becoming dry. These kingdoms are not merely entries on a timeline. They reveal what human authority becomes when it is severed from worship, humility, and justice. The chapter teaches the mind to follow history and the conscience to distrust beastly power.","The sequence teaches more than history. Kingdoms may differ in culture, language, and administration, yet the same question follows them all: will power submit to God or become beastly? Daniel trains the conscience to measure power by heaven's judgment, not by earthly success."],"explanation":"Four great beasts come up from the sea, each different from the others. Babylon dominates Daniel's prophetic horizon, beginning with Nebuchadnezzar's early campaign against Jerusalem and ending when the city falls to Medo-Persia. Daniel lived through that entire transition. The lion is not distant classroom history for him; it is the empire that took him from Judah and tried to rename his life.","The humanizing of the lion gives the symbol a theological edge. Daniel 4 has already shown Nebuchadnezzar becoming beastlike through pride and being restored to reason when he lifts his eyes to heaven. Daniel 7 compresses that lesson into the lion: empire becomes beastly when it forgets God, and humanity is restored only under the rule of the Most High.","Babylon's lion-like majesty makes the later judgment more solemn. This was not a weak empire. It was the kingdom that conquered Jerusalem, carried away the vessels of the house of God, trained Daniel and his friends, and boasted in its splendor. Yet even Babylon's golden greatness is temporary. The lion stands first, but it does not stand forever.","The plucked wings also remind the reader that God can stop the speed of conquest. The Most High does not destroy humanity by humbling it; He restores humanity by bringing it back under His rule.","Babylon warns every strong person and institution. The image is heavy, uneven, and consuming.","This kingdom is Medo-Persia, which replaces Babylon in 539 B.C. and dominates until 331 B.C., when Greek power breaks Persia at the decisive Battle of Gaugamela. Daniel 8:20 names the ram as the kings of Media and Persia, and the ram's unequal horns help explain the bear raised on one side: Persia became the stronger side of the dual power.","The three ribs picture conquest already in the beast's mouth. They fit the devouring expansion of Medo-Persia, commonly connected with major conquered regions such as Lydia, Babylon, and Egypt. The command to devour much flesh reminds the reader that even a kingdom used in providence can still be violent and imperial.","Medo-Persia's unevenness is important. The kingdom is dual, but not equal. Daniel 8's ram has two horns, with one higher than the other and the higher coming up last. That matches the historical movement in which Persia became the dominant side of the Medo-Persian power. The bear raised on one side is a visual way of saying the same thing.","The three ribs should be handled as a broad conquest image rather than a point for needless speculation. The bear has prey in its mouth, and Medo-Persia did devour widely. Major victories and absorbed territories such as Babylon, Lydia, and Egypt fit the picture well. The command to devour much flesh reminds the reader that a kingdom can be used to fulfill God's word against Babylon and still be judged as an empire of conquest.","The bear's command to devour much flesh should be read in the shadow of Babylon's fall. Medo-Persia becomes an instrument by which God ends Babylon's dominion, yet the bear is still a beast. That tension is important. A kingdom may fulfill part of God's providential purpose and still remain accountable for violence, ambition, and conquest.","The raised side also explains why the kingdom is often named Medo-Persia rather than simply Media or Persia. The two elements belong together, but Persia rises to greater prominence. Daniel 8 later makes this clearer through the ram whose higher horn comes up last. Scripture gives enough internal evidence for the identification without asking the reader to lean on guesswork.","The fall of Babylon and rise of Medo-Persia show that no empire is permanent. God can use one kingdom to end another, yet every devouring power remains accountable to Him."],"explanation":"The second beast is like a bear. After Alexander's death, his empire fractured under successor powers rather than passing intact to one heir. Greece's dominion shapes the world into which Rome will rise and in which the gospel will later be preached.","The four heads are a mercy in the interpretation because they show that the prophecy expected division after Alexander. This is the kind of detail that strengthens confidence in the Bible's prophetic outline.","Speed can be intoxicating. Greece rises quickly, dazzles the world, and fragments. Daniel reminds us that momentum is not the same thing as permanence. Its prophetic dominance is counted from 168 B.C., when Rome decisively subdues Greek power, to A.D. 476, when the Western Roman imperial structure collapses. The iron teeth echo the iron legs of Daniel 2: Rome breaks, crushes, organizes, and tramples with unusual force.","The ten horns show that Rome does not simply disappear. Daniel 7:24 says the horns are kings arising from the fourth kingdom. After A.D. 476 the western empire fragments into multiple powers. Daniel 2 describes the post-Roman stage more generally as divided iron-and-clay feet; Daniel 7 supplies the separate ten-horn detail. The iron and clay also point to an attempted mingling of civil and religious power that cannot truly hold.","Rome's fulfillment is historically weighty because the fourth beast is not simply another animal in the set. Daniel cannot name a known creature for it. Its iron teeth link it back to Daniel 2's iron kingdom, and its trampling character fits Rome's military, legal, and administrative dominance. Rome's rise to supremacy over the Greek world is marked here from 168 B.C., with its western imperial structure collapsing in A.D. 476.","The ten horns should not be confused with the toes of Daniel 2. Daniel 2's feet and toes emphasize a divided post-Roman stage, with iron and clay showing strength mixed with brittleness and an unnatural church-state kind of mingling. Daniel 7 is the chapter that explicitly gives ten horns and then interprets them as kings arising from the fourth kingdom. That distinction keeps the symbols honest.","This verse also prepares for the little horn by showing that Rome continues in divided form. The western empire collapses politically, but Roman influence does not disappear. The divided landscape after A.D. 476 becomes the historical soil in which a different kind of power can rise.","The fourth beast's iron teeth connect Daniel 7 with Daniel 2 without collapsing every symbol into the same meaning. Daniel 2's iron legs identify Rome's strength, and its feet of iron mixed with clay show a later divided and mixed condition. Daniel 7 uses ten horns when it wants to speak of kings arising from Rome. The feet and toes show division and mixture; the horns supply the more specific post-Roman powers.","The iron-and-clay background is still useful because it explains the kind of world in which the little horn rises. After imperial Rome fragments, civil strength remains, but it is mixed with a religious element that tries to bind what God never made into one coercive kingdom. Daniel 7 then narrows the focus to the horn that uses religious claims with political force.","The fourth beast is a warning about power without mercy. Rome's strength was real, but strength is not righteousness. Daniel wants God's people to know that even the most terrifying empire will stand before the court of heaven."],"explanation":"The fourth beast is unlike the others. The little horn rises from the fourth beast, and the fourth beast is Rome. It appears among the ten horns, so it arises in the divided Roman world after A.D. 476. It comes after the ten, differs from them, uproots three, speaks against God, persecutes the saints, and later dominates for the 1260-year period of Daniel 7:25.","$14","The three uprooted horns belong to the historical rise of papal supremacy. The Heruli, Vandals, and Ostrogoths are commonly identified because these powers stood in the way of the religious-political dominance that later centered in Rome. Their removal was not the whole story of papal ascent, but it fits Daniel's mark that three of the first horns are plucked up before the little horn.","The horn's eyes and mouth tell us the kind of power we are dealing with. Eyes suggest intelligence, oversight, and claims to insight; the mouth speaks great things, later explained as words against the Most High. This is not merely a military invader. It is a religious-political power that teaches, claims authority, defines obedience, and reaches into conscience.","Revelation 13 confirms the same profile by describing a beast that receives a mouth speaking great things and blasphemies, continues for forty-two months, and makes war with the saints. Daniel gives the root prophecy; Revelation expands the final form. The point is not hostility toward individual believers, but fidelity to Scripture over any system that places human authority above God's Word.","The identification is strongest when the marks are kept together. No single mark carries the whole identification by itself, but the whole profile is much more specific: Roman setting, post-Roman timing, rise among divided western powers, difference from ordinary kingdoms, three uprooted powers, blasphemous claims, persecution, attempted change of God's law, and the 1260-year period. The biblical evidence points to a system, not merely one immoral ruler.","Antiochus IV fails especially at the points Daniel emphasizes. He belongs to the Greek line before Rome, while Daniel's little horn rises from the fourth beast after Rome divides. He does not arise among the ten horns of the fourth kingdom, does not fit the 1260-year period, and does not account for Revelation's later use of the same blasphemous, persecuting, forty-two-month pattern. Daniel 8 will need careful study on its own terms, but Daniel 7's little horn is Roman and post-Roman.","The dates A.D. 538-1798 are used because they match the rise and wounding of papal supremacy in history. The beginning marks the point at which papal authority gains decisive civil standing after opposing powers are cleared away; the end marks the dramatic blow to papal political power when the pope is taken captive in 1798. The dates do not exhaust the prophecy, but they fit the time, place, character, and action of the horn.","Daniel is not attacking sincere believers inside any communion; he is exposing a system that claims authority over conscience and God's law. Babylon wanted all peoples, nations, and languages gathered before its image. Babylon falls in 539 B.C., Medo-Persia falls in 331 B.C., Greece yields to Rome in 168 B.C., and Western Rome collapses in A.D. 476. The Son of Man's kingdom is not next in the same fragile chain. It is of another order.","This gives prophecy its worshipful center. The future does not belong to beasts, horns, tribunals, armies, or persecuting systems. Daniel does not decide what the symbols mean by preference. He asks, and the interpretation comes from heaven. The horn belongs to the fourth beast, so the setting is Roman rather than Greek.\n2. It rises among the ten horns, narrowing the scene to divided Western Rome.\n3. Its reach affects the Mediterranean world, the Jewish nation, and the setting of Christ's first coming.","Rome is different because its strength is not only military. In Daniel 7, that power becomes the beast from which the ten horns and little horn arise.","The fourth kingdom devours the whole earth in the sense of the prophetic world it dominates. Rome's reach across the Mediterranean, its military roads, legal structures, taxation, and imperial order made it unlike the earlier powers. Daniel is describing not only conquest but a system that grinds down resistance.","Rome's connection to Daniel 2 should remain firm. Daniel 8:14 and Daniel 9 provide the prophetic line that points to 1844 as the beginning of the heavenly judgment phase.","This verse also keeps the final destruction future. A loss of dominion in history is not the same as the complete end of beastly power. That is why Revelation's later scenes still matter. God begins judgment in heaven before the whole system is visibly consumed at the end.","This sequence helps prevent two opposite errors. It also prevents carelessness, because the final destruction is still future and Revelation warns of renewed beastly influence before the end. Daniel 7 gives a sober, watchful hope.","The judgment is therefore not an optional appendix to prophecy. It is the turning point between oppression and inheritance. It takes away the daily, and the place of His sanctuary is cast down. This is one of the most solemn verses in the chapter because the conflict now reaches Christ's authority and sanctuary truth.","The Prince of the host is the heavenly Commander and covenant Lord of God's people. Rome's opposition to the Prince includes pagan Rome's role in Christ's crucifixion and papal Rome's later obscuring of His priestly ministry.","The phrase 'the daily' has been understood in two major ways. One reading sees it as paganism, the continual pagan opposition that is removed or transformed as papal Rome rises. The immediate prophetic flow gives that reading a slight contextual advantage: Daniel 8 moves from pagan Rome's horizontal conquest into a later religious-political phase, and history shows pagan imperial forms giving way to papal supremacy. The horn does not begin as a church power; it develops through Rome's transition.","A second reading sees the daily as Christ's continual heavenly ministry, obscured by counterfeit priesthood, sacrifice, mediation, confession, and worship. That reading takes seriously the sanctuary setting, the reference to the Prince, and Hebrews' emphasis that Christ ministers in the heavenly sanctuary. It rightly keeps the issue centered on Christ rather than on political history alone.","The case for the paganism reading may be stated plainly:\n1. The vision moves from pagan Rome's military expansion to a later religious-political phase, so the daily can mark the pagan form that is taken away in the transition.\n2. Daniel 8 and Daniel 11 both show powers shifting through history rather than introducing an isolated sanctuary term without context.\n3. The rise of papal supremacy required the displacement, absorption, and transformation of pagan imperial Rome.\n4. The phrase works naturally in the chapter's movement from horizontal conquest to vertical religious claims.\n5. This reading explains why the same Roman horn can begin in pagan imperial power and later act through papal authority.","The case for Christ's continual ministry also deserves respect:\n1. Daniel 8 is a sanctuary chapter, so the daily can be connected with continual priestly service.\n2. The horn magnifies itself to the Prince, which draws attention to Christ's heavenly work.\n3. Hebrews teaches that Christ ministers continually in the heavenly sanctuary.\n4. Papal mediation, priesthood, repeated sacrifice, and human absolution do obscure the believer's direct confidence in Christ.\n5. This reading makes the devotional burden of the verse strongly Christ-centered.","Saying that the daily is taken away does not mean Christ's actual ministry in heaven could be stopped by an earthly power. No horn can remove Jesus from the right hand of God. The issue is historical and experiential: a system can remove the knowledge of His ministry from the people's view, replace confidence in His direct mediation with human mediation, and train consciences to look away from the heavenly sanctuary.","That is why the paganism reading and the sanctuary application belong together. The immediate prophetic movement explains the historical transition from pagan Rome to papal Rome. The sanctuary setting explains what the papal phase does once it rises: it obscures Christ's continual ministry. One reading explains the transition of power; the other preserves the Christ-centered burden of the conflict.","The verse is therefore not a technical debate detached from devotion. It asks where the sinner is taught to look. If a system directs the conscience to human priests, repeated sacrifices, earthly absolution, or institutional authority instead of Christ's heavenly priesthood, it has cast a shadow over the daily confidence believers should have in Jesus.","The two readings do not need to be treated as enemies. The prophetic flow slightly favors paganism as the power displaced in the rise of papal Rome, while the papal phase clearly obscures Christ's true continual ministry. The practical burden of the verse is therefore Christ-centered: no earthly priesthood, ritual system, or religious authority may stand between the soul and the heavenly ministry of Jesus."],"explanation":"The little horn magnifies itself even to the Prince of the host. This is the center of Daniel 8. The trampling of truth, sanctuary, and God's people will not last forever. God appoints an end point for the vision and promises cleansing, restoration, and vindication.","The word translated 'cleansed' carries the idea of being restored, justified, or set right. Leviticus 16 gives the earthly pattern through the Day of Atonement, when the sanctuary was cleansed from the sins confessed over it and the camp was brought into solemn judgment and restoration. Daniel 8 lifts that sanctuary language into the prophetic future.","Hebrews shows why this matters after the cross. Christ is the true High Priest, ministering in the heavenly sanctuary, not in a man-made copy. His sacrifice is complete, yet His priestly ministry applies, intercedes, cleanses, and brings the plan of salvation to its appointed conclusion. Daniel 8:14 is not about rebuilding confidence in an earthly ritual system; it points to God's heavenly answer through Christ.","The 2300 evenings-mornings follow the year-day principle used in prophetic time, where symbolic days represent years. Numbers 14:34 and Ezekiel 4:6 show the principle, and Daniel's own time prophecies require it. The time cannot be a literal six-year period if the vision reaches the time of the end and includes the whole sweep from Medo-Persia through Greece to Rome's later religious phase.","Daniel 8 gives the length but not the starting point. Daniel 9 returns to the same prophet, the same concern about sanctuary and desolation, and the same angel Gabriel. There the seventy weeks are 'cut off' for Daniel's people and city, beginning with the decree to restore and build Jerusalem. Ezra 7 records the decree in 457 B.C. Counting the 2300 years from that point reaches 1844.","The connection with Daniel 9 is essential because Gabriel does not finish explaining the time element in Daniel 8. When he returns, he tells Daniel to consider the vision and marks off the seventy weeks from the larger period.","The seventy weeks also anchor the prophecy in Christ. From 457 B.C., the sixty-nine weeks reach to the appearance of Messiah the Prince in A.D. 27. In the midst of the seventieth week, Christ causes sacrifice and oblation to cease by His death, not by destroying the value of worship but by fulfilling the sacrificial system. The time prophecy therefore begins with restoration, centers in Messiah, and extends to sanctuary cleansing.","The decree in 457 B.C. is significant because it does more than allow a return. It restores Jerusalem's civil and religious order under God's law, giving the kind of starting point Daniel 9 describes. From that date, seventy prophetic weeks, or 490 years, reach to the Messiah-centered events of A.D. 27, A.D. 31, and A.D. 34. Those fulfilled markers give confidence for the longer 2300-year line.","Counting from 457 B.C. to 1844 must also remember that there is no year zero between B.C. and A.D. The shorter seventy-week prophecy is fulfilled in Christ with precision, and that shorter period belongs to the larger 2300-year framework. The same starting point that leads to Messiah also leads to the sanctuary cleansing.","The 1844 connection is not an isolated date added to the chapter. Daniel 7 shows the heavenly court after the little horn's period and before the kingdom is received. Daniel 8 gives the sanctuary cleansing. Daniel 9 supplies the starting framework and anchors the prophecy in Messiah's work. Together they point to a heavenly judgment and cleansing phase in Christ's ministry.","The Day of Atonement background makes the meaning clearer. In Leviticus 16 the sanctuary is cleansed after sin has been dealt with through sacrifice and priestly ministry. The people are called to affliction of soul, repentance, and complete dependence on God's provision. Daniel 8 points to the heavenly reality: God sets right His sanctuary, His truth, His people, and His public government before the final kingdom.","Hebrews keeps this from becoming a cold calculation. Christ has offered one perfect sacrifice, has entered the heavenly sanctuary, and ministers as the High Priest who can save to the uttermost. The judgment connected with this verse is not a denial of the gospel; it is the gospel brought into its final heavenly phase, where Christ's sacrifice, mediation, cleansing, and verdict are shown to be sufficient.","This also guards against misunderstanding 1844 as a date for Christ's return to earth. Daniel 7 shows the Son of Man coming to the Ancient of Days in heaven. Daniel 8 speaks of the sanctuary being cleansed.
